She is a trial attorney specializing in domestic violence and sex trafficking victims. She reports that lately her symptoms have been impacting her work productivity. I want another opinion.   The Search for Answers: Are Private Prisons Superior to Public Prisons?   Beverly Siegfried Department of Criminal Justice, The University of Arkansas at Little Rock CRJU 4304: Criminal Justice Research Dr. Suzanne Coble November 20.2022   The Search for Answers: Are Private Prisons Superior to Public Prisons?   Private prisons in the United States profit from mass imprisonment and claim to save money; also, they claim lower recidivism rates than state prisons (Selman &amp; Leighton, 2010). This claim is being challenged (Reisig &amp; Clear, 2022, p. 5), and one needs to determine if for-profit prisons are the best way to help prisoners compared to public prisons. Furthermore, I must decide whether they supply less, equal, or superior services to the prisoners. People's lives are involved, from the prisoners to their families, and the need for investigation is significant due to the national incarceration industry, which is a risk factor to the well-being of the inmates and their families (Selman &amp; Leighton, 2010, p. 5). Research Topics and Questions Topic Are private prisons superior to public prisons? Research Questions Do private prisons offer better services to prisoners than federal and state-run prisons? Do private prisons save taxpayers money? Is there a risk of prioritizing money over the prisoner's well-being? Relevance and importance Private prisons in the United States are profiting from the mass incarceration dilemma with claims of saving money and lowering recidivism rates. This claim is being challenged, and I need to determine if for-profit prisons are the best way to help prisoners with the financial difficulties of public prisons effectively. Additionally, I must decide whether they supply less, equal, or superior services to the prisoners. People's lives are involved, from the prisoners to their families, and the need for examination is significant. Research and Design The first approach would be to compare previous studies on public and private prisons to determine if for-profit prisons are effective. While doing this, I would develop a similar Liker Scale survey for wardens from private and from public prisons to determine the programs offered and the quality of services provided for private and public prisons. Measuring Concepts of Interest As there is conflicting research, I will need to perform a quantitative study collecting and evaluating non-numerical data to identify conceptions or biased opinions. I will need to compare their methods and determine if they are helpful in conjunction with my research. I will then determine if my survey is consistent with previous research. My independent variable will be the wardens from privately owned prisons and federal and state prisons; my dependent variables will be programs provided, thepercentage of inmates participating in the programs,the racial makeup, location, size of the prison, title of who completed the survey, number of inmates housed, thepopulation of prisoners, and the belief of superiority. My variables will affect the study and will be used to categorize research subjects. I will need to determine the financial performance, social responsibility, and service quality in private and public prisons.   Potential Challenges A significant challenge could be finding enough similar research, as the questions must be similar while I must be mindful of any bias in their study. Recruiting participants for the survey will also be a considerable challenge.  Literature Review of Sources Punishment For Sale: Private Prisons, Big Business, And the Incarceration Binge By Donna Selman &amp; Paul Leighton Hypothesis This study aims to determine if the money-making prison has distorted the goals of private prisons and harmed prisoners.  As of today, private prisons have failed to prove that they are superior to state prisons and may cease to exist. The methods will involve analyzing the responses of prison wardens from privately owned and publicly owned prisons. Research Questions The research question provides the social, political, and economic context for the two main drivers of modern-day private prisons and a comprehensive view of private prisons as businesses (Selman &amp; Leighton, 2010, p. 5). Moreover, it determines if private prisons are money-making machines and have failed to produce what they promised. Moreover, it questions whether private prisons, shareholders, and lending institutions depend on continuing these procedures for revenue and growth (p. 139). Research Questions The authors want to study the free market experiment in punishment, its effects, and a critical analysis of the private prisons and developments that increased them (p. 5). Findings The authors believe that big money has created a money-making machine and identifies the schemes on earnings from marginalized populations that indicate future growth for the private prisons industry. They determine that the political, economic, and cultural conditions are in danger of increasing privatization; moreover, the authors claim privatization and the methods used to promote prison populations are evolving with a plan to turn more marginalized people into lucrative commodities. Moreover, local, state and federal governments struggle with funding and decisions about the growing correctional system (p. 151). Their goal is to provide a thorough assessment of the \"political, cultural, historical, and economic entanglements of privatization\" (Selman &amp; Leighton, 2010, p. 152) and to understand the response from our nation (p. 152) Strengths The strengths of this study are the process of reviewing previous studies and data to build their argument. The authors gave a complete history of private prisons and did an excellent job finding good research. Weaknesses The biggest weakness is that the data is twelve years old; secondly, the authors state, \"our opinions and conclusions are our own\" (Selman &amp; Leighton, 2010, p. 6). Implications  This book implies that private prisons grew from mass incarceration, which wasted taxpayer money, negatively affected crime, and promoted racial injustice. Privatization is supposed to provide superior services at a lower cost. Nevertheless, private prisons have billions of dollars invested in them, which depends on continual growth for the revenue needed (Selman &amp; Leighton, 2010, p. 163). Privatizing prisons has made mass incarceration a vast money-making machine. More studies are required to determine if the companies that run the prisons are superior to state prisons or just into making money.  Serving the Public Good? A Corpus-Assisted Discourse Analysis of Private Prisons and For-Profit Incarceration in the United States by Karoline Marko Research Questions  The evaluation and explanation of public and private communications, such as the conversations these companies administer, and the discourse on their websites, with two of the leading private prison organizations in the United States (Marko, 2021). Findings Private prisons are based on a for-profit philosophy that tries to conserve money by cutting costs. Based on their profit orientation, they have no interest in rehabilitating offenders due to making money with every prisoner. This study explores the concerns from a linguistic perspective and takes a mass number of texts collected from the websites of two central private prisons. She claims that the investigated corporations shift the conversational focus from negativity by concentrating on violence and recidivism and switch to a positive dialogue that centers on reentry and safety. These theories contradict what many researchers and journalists have discovered (Marko, 2021). Strengths The author takes a unique approach to understand the corporations' thoughts on the background issues of violence, recidivism, and costs with a large amount of naturally occurring texts and analyzing with a corpus program to understand actual linguistic usage. Therefore, exploring significant themes in the dataset and focusing on the \"safety and security of their facilities and their reentry programs for inmates\" (Marko, 2021). Also, this is up-to-date research and covers many issues with private prisons. Weaknesses The study only used two significant corporations for private prisons to get the data, and the author is the one that determines what is being expressed. Implications This study reveals that companies are conscious of the issues and issues raised by scholars and the media; however, they spin the information in a way that will help a positive self-report. Addressing violence/safety/security and recidivism/reentry to a certain extent, they prefer presenting a positive assessment and choosing the most favorable angle. There is a discrepancy and contradiction between public and private dialogues (Marko, 2021).  Staff-Prisoner Relationships, Staff Professionalism, and the Use of Authority In Public- and Private-Sector Prisons by Ben Crewe, Allison Leibling, and Susie Hulley Research Questions This article compares and clarifies three domains of prison culture and quality, the relationships between the staff and prisoners, the levels of staff professionalism, and the prisoners' involvement with state authority. They will identify public and private prisons' characteristics, strengths, and weaknesses (Crewe et al., 2015, p. 309). Findings  The survey-based comparison of matched prisons and the public-sector institutions surpassed the private-sector comparisons on the prisoners' professed quality of life (Crewe et al., 2015, p. 318). Strengths  This survey gives an in-depth look into prisoners' perceptions of private and public prisons. The study also shows the connection between the prisoner and staff attitudes. Weaknesses  This study cannot assure that you can generalize the performance and practices of private prisons, general or specific, in the areas that are the most pertinent in debates on the impact of the privatization of public services. Additionally, this study was done on European prisons and may not translate well in the United States.  Implications  The research shows that there is not a straightforward relationship between the profit purpose or public service and worth. In the public sphere, even though a set of principles that include decency, integrity, and social justice are declared at senior levels, many of the staff do not follow this agenda, and in supervising prisons, it translates into practice. The data shows that staff is frequently confident and knowledgeable, delivering safe and reliable methods and exercising power fairly and consistently. These attitudes help to counteract the staff's attitudes toward prisoners, which are often retaliatory, disrespectful, or unsympathetic. In the private sector, the status quo is nearly the antithesis. Profit is essential for private sector participation and is the most crucial dialogue at senior levels within global corporations competing to operate prisons (Crewe et al., 2015, p. 333).  Does Authoritarianism Breed Corruption? Reconsidering the Relationship Between Authoritarian Governance and Corrupt Exchanges in Bureaucracies by Marina Zaloznaya Research Questions This intention is to imply that repressive power breeds bureaucratic corruption. Secondly, the intention of support that dictatorial elites allow corruption to thrive in trade for the political devotion of the people. Does Authoritarianism Breed Corruption? Does Authoritarianism Breed Bureaucratic Corruption? Does Political Loyalty Mediate the Effect of Autocracy On Bureaucratic Corruption? (Zaloznaya, 2015, pp. 346-7). Findings The data in this study discovers regular differences in corrupt governmental operations with a different governmental segment in a specific dictatorship.   Strengths The author uses qualitative data and advocates for the ethnographic and historical study of the political roots of corruption. She is very thorough in her study. Weaknesses  This study is on one country only and can only be used generally towards prisons as they are separate entities where power often corrupts those working there. Implications This article suggests that ethnographic and historical research methods generate a more accurate perception of the political roots of corruption than quantitative or theoretical work. Moreover, seek to prove it. These approaches allow for analytic differences between political and governmental corruption. It also suggests that authoritarian governance can generate various opportunities, desires, and cultural inclinations toward corruption across different sectors. Interestingly, the results suggest that political loyalty does facilitate the link between authoritarianism and corruption but only when an autocrat understands and is afraid of the disloyalty. Therefore, this study provokes several essential questions that have not been counted by anticorruption scholars (Zaloznaya, 2015, p. 345). Inmate Recidivism as Measure of Private Prison Performance by Andrew L. Spivak and Susan F. Sharp Research Questions  The authors want to assess and evaluate the success of private prisons in meeting the demands of the state and the prisoner by evaluating the cost and post-release occurrences. They also compare the private industry's services in quality to those of the public sector (Spivak &amp; Sharp, 2000, p. 483). Findings This study offers several augmentations to the current literature on private versus public prison recidivism. Their methods improved on those in other studies by implementing a quasi-experimental approach of multiple private and public inmate group categories that took the amount and proportion of time expended in each type of facility. The authors' conservative analysis of these results indicates that serving more time in private prisons does not seem to improve an inmate's post-release performance. Private prison programs are purported to accomplish higher participation and completion rates; however, future studies need to obtain data on inmate programs between private and public facilities. Convicts might validate or invalidate this assertion and could also reveal the role of programming on the inmates' post-release performance (Spivak &amp; Sharp, 2000, p. 504). Strengths  This study was comprehensive, built upon previous research, and used reliable methods for data collection. Weaknesses  Although this study is dated, it can be used as a reference point for future research. This research is limited to obtaining most of its data from Florida, and this study includes only grown persons and not juveniles. Additionally, recidivating is limited to the operational definition and does not consider rearrest. Their selection can impact and influence group variations and comparisons. Implications Interestingly, this study adds augmentations to existing bodies of literature on whether the private versus public prison recidivism rate claims are valid. Their methods build on and improve previous research while determining that future policy decisions should concentrate on comparative expense issues. Likewise, the analyses found no proof that private prisons are superior to public prison inmate recidivism. The study found that spending a significant proportion of time served shows a modest increase in recidivism. Those who spent a higher percentage of their time in public prisons had a modestly higher chance of recidivism (Spivak &amp; Sharp, 2000, p. 501). Research Proposal: Hypothesis Purpose Of This Study The purpose of the current study is to determine if private prisons are superior to state prisons or if they are money-making endeavors at the expense of the prisoners. The methods will be to analyze data on the cost-effectiveness of private prisons and surveys given to incarcerated prisoners and wardens to determine the level of services provided, including data on state prison services. This study can assess the quality of services and determine which type of prison is superior. Current Study Hypotheses First, private prisons are money makers, and the system suffers from this attitude, which in turn is detrimental to the prisoners by not allocating funds for inmate services that are needed desperately by those incarcerated. It is hypothesized that private prisons are likelier to spend less on services per prisoner than state prisons. When the prison population is seen as moneymakers, the prisoners are in jeopardy of not receiving proper care and services. Sadly, when making money is the primary goal and service is secondary, the inmates of private prisons suffer. The author regularly hears reports on sub-standard facilities, food, lack of employees, and overcrowding, all of which affect the safety and well-being of all incarcerated or working. Secondly, private prisons may not save money for the state as a higher recidivism rate could cost the system more in the long run. Prisons aim to punish and rehabilitate offenders, and I must wonder if rehabilitation is occurring suitably with privately owned prisons. This can answer the question of whether or not the claims that private prisons are superior to publicly owned prisons are valid. Methods Proposed sample Private prisons in the United States imprisoned 115,428 people in 2019, representing about 8% of the state and federal prison populace. Subsequently, since 2000, this number has increased to 32%, whereas the prison population only rose by 3% (Zoby, 2021). For this proposed sample, the target population is the wardens of 158 private prisons and the wardens of 158 federal and state prisonsto determine whether or not the claims that private prisons are superior to publicly owned prisons.To establish the answers, we will analyze data on the cost-effectiveness of each prison to ascertain the level of services provided, the cost of housing inmates, and the effectiveness of the programs; to answer the question of superiority. Each warden will be given an online probability sample. In selecting the group of subjects for this study, the author will select part of a population in each prison that is similar in nature and size. The author will select samples to determine the cost-effectiveness of private prisons compared to federal and state-run prisons, the effectiveness of their programs, determine what percentage of participation is in these programs, and determine which prisons offer better programs for the incarcerated. The wardens will be asked to participate in each prison, and 300 will be chosen from corresponding prisons who respond. I have defined a line of questions: Question one: How much does it cost to house each inmate? Question two: What programs do you provide for the inmates? Question three: What percentage of inmates participate in these programs? Question four: How many inmates complete each program? Question five: Do you believe your prison is superior to the private or federal and state prisons (yes or no)? Question six: What is the racial makeup of your facility? Question seven: Where is your prison located? Question eight: How many inmates are housed in your facility? Question nine: What population of prisoners is incarcerated there (male, female, juvenile)? Question ten: What is the title of who is responding? Each answer will be given a numerical value to see how the prisons compare. Content Analysis A web-based survey will be sent to the respondents, who will be asked to click on a link that eventually takes them to an anonymous questionnaire application. Thesurvey ratings will be structured to provide measurable reaction collections to make the data easy to analyze. The respondents will have various simple answers about their private, federal, or state-run prisons. Variables and Measures The variable under consideration is the respondents' answers and attitude toward their prison. This is a random sampling variable. This variable may be logically rank ordered, are ordinal measure. The different attributes represent relatively more or less of the variable (Maxfield &amp; Babbie, 2016, p. 87). To choose my sample, I will randomly assign each person a number and use a computer program to generate 100 random numbers (Maxfield &amp; Babbie, 2016, pp. 151-3). Potential Implications and Limitations Based on this study, future researchers will be able to determine whether or not the claims that private prisons are superior to publicly owned prisons. To find an answer to the question, this research will contact the wardens of every private adult prison (federal and state) in the United States, as well as an equal number of publicly owned state and federal adult prisons that house comparable populations. Along with questions on basic demographics, the wardens will be asked whether they feel that private prisons are preferable to state or federal prisons and why they hold this belief. The information will be collected by surveying wardens of privately owned and publicly owned prisons with similar characteristics to the respective institutions, as well as whether or not they feel their institution is better. Even though most people who are incarcerated in this country are held in facilities that are owned and operated by the government, there is a small percentage of inmates (approximately 8%) who are housed in facilities that are run by private companies and are in the business of making money (Wagner, 2015). Consistent research must be carried out and developed upon the findings of this study. Implications This study is essential to develop quality criminal justice policies regarding the continuation and the quality of private, for-profit prisons. This study's results could further our understanding of private versus public prisons. If one is superior to the other, it might be applied to creating new interpositions or regulatory measures. Moreover, the study will seek to assess if there is a better way for mass incarceration to proceed in locking up inmates. There has been much debate on whether prisons should be for profit. Most people incarcerated in this country are confined in publicly owned prisons; however, a faction of inmates is in facilities operated by private companies under contract with states or the federal government. Are private prisons a way to profit from America's mass imprisonment, and are they encouraging mass incarceration? These questions must be studied to determine if private prisons live up to the hype the corporations running these businesses give. Consequently, this study can be built upon by future researchers to determine if private prisons are superior to publicly owned prisons, as they claim. This study will try to answer the question by contacting wardens at every private prison in the United States with an equal amount of state and federal prisons with similar populations. The wardens will be asked whether they believe private prisons are superior to state or federal prisons and why along with basic demographics. The information can be obtained by sampling wardens from private and publicly owned prisons about their prison conditions, attributes, and if they believe their prison is superior to the other. Even if most people imprisoned in this country are confined to publicly owned prisons, a small minority of people (about 8%) reside in facilities under contract and operated by private companies involved with money-making procedures (Wagner, 2015). Regular studies need to be conducted and built upon this study to determine if we are trending toward more or less private prisons while establishing the quality of these prisons. Limitations This proposed study assesses whether wardens feel private prisons are superior to publicly owned ones. Accordingly, comparing private and public prisons should be as similar as possible concerning the design and volume of inmates, security level, and different types of inmates. Alternatively, any qualified evaluation of service superiority could be distorted (GAO Report, 1996, p. 3). Another factor that should be considered in the interstate evaluations of correctional facilities is the level to which theAmerican Correctional Association accredits the prison. Acquiring ACA accreditation indicates that the prison meets the minimum standards and can be problematic when the prisons are in separate states. Each state and its prison system have different characteristics and conditions that must be acknowledged when conducting regional assessments (GAO Report, 1996, p. 6). Besides, exploring whether the offender is viewed as corrupt and who threatens the victim and society could impact the incarceration rate and influence the function and approach, which might change the expectation of service (GAO Report, 1996, p. 7).
